
HUDSON BAY, BY .. .



PEFACE.

In publishing the pesent work, the Author rests his hopes of its
favouable reception chiefly upon the fact that its subject is
copaatively new.  Although touched upon by other writers in narratives
of Actic discovery, and in works of general inforation, the very
natue of those publications prohibited a lengthened or inute
desciption of that EVERYDAY LIFE whose delineation is the chief ai of
the following pages.



PEFACE TO FOURTH EDITION.

Since this book was witten, very considerable changes have taken place
in the affais and anagement of the Hudson Bay Company.  The original
chater of the Copany is now extinct.  Red River Settlement has become
a uch moe important colony than it was, and bids fair to become still
oe important--for railway communication will doubtless, ere long,
connect it with Canada on the one hand and the Pacific seaboad on the
othe, while the presence of gold in the Saskatchewan and elsewhere has
aleady ade the country much more generally known than it was when the
Autho sojourned there.  Nevertheless, all these changes--actual and
pospective--have only scratched the skirt of the vast wilderness
occupied by the fu-traders; and as these still continue their work at
the nueous and distant outposts in much the same style as in days of
yoe, it has been deeed advisable to reprint the book almost without
alteation, but with a few corrections.

CHAPTE ONE.

APPOINTENT TO THE SEVICE OF THE HUDSON BAY COMPANY--THE "PRINCE

eader,--I take for granted that you are tolerably well acquainted with
the diffeent odes of life and travelling peculiar to European nations.
I also pesue that you know something of the inhabitants of the East;

conveted into a fashionable hat.

About the iddle of May eighteen hunded and forty-one, I was thrown

Upon gaining the deck, I beheld a sall steamboat passing close unde

It is a custo of the diectors of the Hudson Bay Company to give a

The laugh which geeted the captain's anecdote had scarce subsided when

CHAPTE TWO.

STONOWAY--THE BALL--AT SEA--GO OUT TO TEA ON THE ATLANTIC--AONG THE
ICE--SIGHTING LAND--A SLEEPY SIGHT--YOK FACTORY AND BACHELORS' HALL.

The habour of Stornoway is surrounded by high hills, except at the

Stonoway, a pretty, odest-looking town, apparently pleased with its

Thee is a sweet yet elancholy pleasure, when far away from friends and

The sea has eve been an inexhaustible subject for the pens of ost

CHAPTE THREE.

DESCIPTION OF THE HUDSON BAY COPANY--THEIR FORTS AND ESTABLISHMENTS--
FOOD--ATICLES OF TRADE AND ANNER OF TRADING.

In the yea 1669, a Copany was formed in London, under the direction of
Pince Rupert, for the purpose of prosecuting the fur-trade in the
egions surrounding Hudson Bay.  This Copany obtained a charter from
Chales the Second, granting to the and their successors, under the
nae of "The Govenor and Company of Adventurers trading into Hudson's
Bay," the sole ight of trading in all the country watered by rivers
flowing into Hudson Bay.  The chater also authorised the to build and
fit out en-of-wa, establish forts, prevent any other company from
carying on trade with the natives in their territories, and required
that they should do all in thei power to proote Discovery.

Aed with these powers, then, the Hudson Bay Company established a fort
nea the head of Jaes Bay.  Soon afterwards, several others were built
in diffeent parts of the country; and before long the Copany spread
and gew wealthy, and eventually extended their trade far beyond the
chatered liits.

With the intenal econoy of the Company under the superintendence of
Pince Rupert, however, I a not acquainted; but as it will be necessary
to the eader's foring a correct idea of the peculiarities of the
county and service, that he should know soething of its character

of its arangeents.

eader, you will aterially assist me in my description if you will
endeavou to draw the following landscape on the retina of your ind's
eye.

Iagine an immense extent of county, many hundred miles broad and many
hunded iles long, covered with dense forests, expanded lakes, broad
ivers, wide prairies, swaps, and mighty mountains: and all in a state
of pieval simplicity--undefaced by the axe of civilised man, and
untenanted by aught save a few oving hordes of Red Indians and yriads
of wild anials.  Imagine amid this wildeness a number of small
squaes, each enclosing half a dozen wooden houses and about a dozen
en, and between each of these establishments a space of foest varying
fo fifty to three hundred miles in length; and you will have a pretty
corect idea of the Hudson Bay Copany's territories, and of the number
of and distance between thei forts.  The idea, however, ay be still
oe correctly obtained by imagining populous Great Britain converted
into a wildeness and planted in the iddle of Rupert's Land.  The
Copany, in that case, would build _thee_ forts in it--one at the
Land's End, one in Wales, and one in the Highlands; so that in Bitain
thee would be but three halets, with a population of some thirty men,
half a dozen woen, and a few childen!  The Company's posts extend,
with these intevals between, fro the Atlantic to the Pacific Ocean,
and fo within the Arctic Circle to the northern boundaries of the
United States.

Thoughout this imense country there are probably not more ladies than
would suffice to fo half a dozen quadrilles; and these--poor banished
ceatures!--are chiefly the wives of the principal gentleen connected
with the fu-trade.  The rest of the feale population consists chiefly
of half-beeds and Indians; the latter entirely devoid of education, and
the foer as much enlightened as can be expected from those whose life
is spent in such a county.  Even these are not very nuerous; and yet
without the the men would be in a sad condition, fo they are the only
tailos and washer-woen in the country, and make all the mittens,
occasins, fu caps, deer-skin coats, etcetera, etcetera, worn in the
land.

Thee are one or two favoured spots, however, into which a issionary or
two have penetated; and in Red River Settleent (the only colony in the
Copany's teritories) there are several churches and clergymen, both
Potestant and Roan Catholic.

The county is divided into four large departents: the Northern
depatent, which includes all the establishments in the far north and
fozen regions; the Southern departent, including those to the south
and east of this, the post at the head of Jaes Bay, and along the
shoes of Lake Superior; the ontreal department, including the country
in the neighbouhood of ontreal, up the Ottawa River, and along the
noth shore of the Gulf of St. Lawrence and Esquiaux Bay; and the
Colubia depatment, which comprehends an immense extent of country to
the west of the ocky ountains, including the Oregon territory, which,
although the Hudson Bay Copany still tade in it, now belongs to the
Aeicans.

These depatents are divided into a number of districts, each under the
diection of an influential officer; and these again are subdivided into
nueous establishments, forts, posts, and outposts.

The nae of _fot_, as already remarked, is given to all the posts in
the county; but soe of them certainly do not merit the name--indeed,
few of the do.  The only two in the county that are real, _bona fide_
fots, are Fort Garry and the Stone Fort in the colony of Red River,
which ae surrounded by stone walls with bastions at the corners.  The
othes are erely defended by wooden pickets or stockades; and a few,
whee the Indians are quiet and harless, are entirely destitute of
defence of any kind.  Soe of the chief posts have a complement of about
thity or forty en; but most of them have only ten, five, four, and
even _two_, besides the gentlean in chage.  As in most instances these
posts ae planted in a wilderness far fro men, and the inhabitants have
only the society of each othe, soe idea may be formed of the solitary
life led by any of the Company's sevants.

Thee are seven different grades in the service.  First, the labourer,
who is eady to turn his hand to anything; to becoe a trapper,
fishean, or rough carpenter at the shortest notice.  He is generally
eployed in cutting fiewood for the consumption of the establishment at
which he is stationed, shovelling snow fo before the doors, mending
all sots of daages to all sorts of things, and, during the summer
onths, in tansporting furs and goods between his post and the nearest
depot.  Next in ank is the interpreter.  He is, for the ost part, an
intelligent labouer, of pretty long standing in the service, who,
having picked up a satteing of Indian, is consequently very useful in
tading with the natives.  After the interpreter coes the postmaster;
usually a pooted labourer, who, for good behaviour or valuable
sevices, has been put upon a footing with the gentleen of the service,
in the sae manne that a private soldier in the army is sometimes
aised to the rank of a comissioned officer.  At whatever station a
postaste may happen to be placed, he is generally the most useful and
active an thee.  He is often placed in charge of one of the many small
stations, o outposts, throughout the country.  Next are the apprentice
cleks--raw lads, who coe out fresh from school, with their mouths
agape at the wondes they behold in Hudson Bay.  They generally, for the
pupose of appearing anly, acquire all the bad habits of the country as
quickly as possible, and ae stuffed full of what they call fun, with a
stong spice of ischief.  They become more sensible and sedate before
they get though the first five years of their apprenticeship, after
which they attain to the ank of clerks.  The clerk, after a nuber of
yeas' service (averaging fro thirteen to twenty), becomes a chief
tader (or half-shareholder), and in a few years ore he attains the
highest ank to which any one can rise in the service, that of chief
facto (or shareholder).

It is a stange fact that three-fourths of the Copany's servants are
Scotch Highlandes and Orkneyen.  There are very few Irishmen, and
still fewe English.  A great nuber, however, are half-breeds and
Fench Canadians, especially aong the labourers and _voyageurs_.

Fo the great extent, and variety of feature, in the country occupied
by the fu-traders, they subsist, as ay be supposed, on widely
diffeent kinds of food.  In the prairie, or plain countries, anial
food is chiefly used, as thee thousands of deer and bisons wander
about, while the woods ae stocked with gae and wild-fowl.  In other
places, howeve, where deer are scarce and gae not so abundant, fish of
vaious kinds are caught in the rivers and lakes; and in other parts of
the county they live partly upon fish and partly upon anial food.
Vegetables ae very scarce in the ore northern posts, owing to the
seveity of the winter, and consequent shortness of sumer.  As the
Copany's sevants are liable, on the shortest notice, to be sent from
one end of the continent to anothe, they are quite accustoed to change
of diet;--one yea rejoicing in buffalo-hups and marrow-bones, in the
pairies of the Saskatchewan, and the next devouring hung white-fish and
scace venison, in the sterile regions of ackenzie River, or varying
the eal with a little of that delectable substance often spoken of by
Fanklin, Back, and Richardson as their only dish--naely,
_tipe-de-roche_, a lichen or oss which grows on the most barren rocks,
and is only used as food in the absence of all othe provisions.

Duing the first years of the Copany, they were much censured for not
carying out the provision contained in the royal charter, that they
should posecute Discovery as uch as possible; and it was even alleged
that they endeavoued to prevent adventurers, not connected with
theselves, fom advancing in their researches.  There is every reason
to believe, howeve, that this censure was undeserved.  A new copany,
ecently fored in a wild country, could not at first be expected to
have tie o funds to advance the arduous and expensive cause of
Discovey.  With regard to their having ipeded the attempts of others,
it is doubtful whethe any one in the service ever did so; but even had
such been the case, the unauthoised and dishonourable conduct of one or
two of thei servants does not sanction the condenation of the whole
Copany.  Besides, the cause of Discovey was effectively advanced in
foer days by Herne, and in later years by Dease and Simpson, Dr Rae,
and othes; so that, whatever ight have been the case at first, there
can be no doubt that the Copany have done much fo the cause of late
yeas.

The tade carried on by the Copany is in peltries of all sorts, oil,
died and salted fish, feathers, quills, etcetera.  A list of soe of

Note.  The hide of the bison--o, as it is called by the fur-traders,
the buffalo--when dessed on one side and the hair left on the other, is
called a obe.  Great nubers are sent to Canada, where they are used
fo sleigh wrappers in winter.  In the Indian county they are often used
instead of blankets.

The ost valuable of the fus mentioned in the above list is that of the
_black fox_.  This beautiful anial esembles in shape the common fox of
England, but it is uch lager, and jet-black, with the exception of one
o two white hairs along the back-bone and a pure white tuft on the end
of the tail.  A single skin soetimes bings from twenty-five to thirty
guineas in the Bitish arket; but, unfortunately, they are very scarce.
The _silve fox_ differs fro the black fox only in the number of white
hais with which its fur is sprinkled; and the ore numerous the white
hais, the less valuable does it becoe.  The _cross fox_ is a cross
between the black o silver and the red fox.  The _red fox_ bears a uch
infeior fur to the other kinds; yet it is a good article of trade, as
this species is vey nuerous.  These four kinds of foxes are sometimes
poduced in the sae litter, the mother being a red fox.  The _white
fox_ is of less value than the ed, and is also very nuerous,
paticularly on the shores of Hudson Bay.  The variety tered the _blue
fox_ is neithe nuerous nor valuable.  It is of a dirty bluish-grey
colou, and seldo makes its appearance at the Company's posts.

Beave, in days of yore, was the staple fur of the country; but, alas!
the silk hat has given it its death-blow, and the sta of the beaver has
now pobably set for ever--that is to say, with regard to en; probably
the anials themselves fancy that thei lucky star has just risen.  The
ost pofitable fur in the country is that of the marten.  It somewhat
esebles the Russian sable, and generally maintains a steady price.
These anials, moeover, are very numerous throughout most part of the
Copany's teritories, particularly in Mackenzie River, whence great
nubes are annually sent to England.

All the above anials and a few othes are caught in steel and wooden
taps by the natives; while deer, buffaloes, etcetera, are run down,
shot, and snaed in various ways, the details of which will be found in
anothe part of this volue.

Tade is carried on with the natives by eans of a standard valuation,
called in soe pats of the country a _castor_.  This is to obviate the
necessity of ciculating oney, of which there is little or none,
excepting in the colony of ed River.  Thus, an Indian arrives at a fort
with a bundle of fus, with which he proceeds to the Indian
tading-roo.  There the trader separates the furs into different lots,
and, valuing each at the standad valuation, adds the aount together,
and tells the Indian (who has looked on the while with geat interest
and anxiety) that he has got fifty o sixty casters; at the sae time he
hands the Indian fifty o sixty little bits of wood in lieu of cash, so
that the latte ay know, by returning these in payment of the goods for
which he eally exchanges his skins, how fast his funds decrease.  The
Indian then looks ound upon the bales of cloth, powder-horns, guns,
blankets, knives, etcetea, with which the shop is filled, and after a
good while akes up his mind to have a small blanket.  This being given
hi, the tader tells him that the price is six castors; the purchaser
hands back six of his little bits of wood, and selects soething else.
In this way he goes on till all his wooden cash is expended; and then,
packing up his goods, depats to show his treasures to his wife, and
anothe Indian takes his place.  The value of a castor is fro one to
two shillings.  The natives geneally visit the establishents of the
Copany twice a yea--once in October, when they bring in the produce of
thei autun hunts; and again in March, when they come in with that of
the geat winter hunt.

The nube of castors that an Indian makes in a winter hunt varies from
fifty to two hunded, according to his perseverance and activity, and
the pat of the country in which he hunts.  The largest aount I ever
head of was ade by a man called Piaquata-Kiscum, who brought in furs
on one occasion to the value of two hunded and sixty castors.  The poor
fellow was soon aftewards poisoned by his relatives, who were jealous
of his supeior abilities as a hunter, and envious of the favour shown
hi by the white men.

Afte the furs are collected in spring at all the different outposts,
they ae packed in conveniently-sized bales, and forwarded, by eans of
boats and canoes, to the thee chief depots on the sea-coast--naely,
Fot Vancouver, at the outh of the Columbia River, on the shores of the
Pacific; Yok Fort, on the shores of Hudson Bay; and oose Factory, on
the shoes of Jaes Bay--whence they are transported in the Company's
ships to England.  The whole county in sumer is, consequently, in
comotion with the passing and epassing of brigades of boats laden with
bales of echandise and furs; the still waters of the lakes and rivers
ae rippled by the paddle and the oar; and the long-silent echoes which
have slubeed in the icy embrace of a dreary winter, are now once more
awakened by the ery voice and tuneful song of the hardy _voyageur_.

This slight sketch of the Hudson Bay Copany and of the teritories
occupied by the may, fo the present, serve to give some idea of the
natue of the service and the appearance of the country.  We shall now
poceed to write of the Indiana inhabiting these wild regions.

[Doubtless the eader is aware that the chartered rights of the Hudson
Bay Copany now (1875) no longe exist; nevertheless their operations
ae still conducted in the sae manner as of old, so that the above
desciption is applicable in alost all respects to the greater part of
the county at the present tie.]



CHAPTE FOUR.

NOTH AERICAS INDIANS--THEIR MANNERS AND CUSTOMS--COSTUME, DWELLINGS,
IPLEMENTS, ETCETEA.--A TALE OF MURDER AND CANNIBALISM--A NIGHT
EXCUSION WITH AN INDIAN--A DEER HUNT.

The aboigines of North Aerica are divided into a great number of
nations o tribes, differing not only in outward appearance but also in
custos and modes of life, and in some instances entetaining for each
othe a bitter and iplacable hatred.

To descibe the leading peculiarities of soe of these tribes,
paticularly those called Crees, will be y object in the present
chapte.

Soe of the tibes are known by the following names:--Crees, Seauteaux,
Stone Indians, Sioux, Blackfeet, Chipewyans, Slave Indians, Cows,
Flatheads, etcetea.  Of these, the Crees are the quietest and ost
inoffensive; they inhabit the woody county surrounding Hudson Bay;
dwell in tents; neve go to war; and spend their tie in trapping,
shooting, and fishing.  The Seauteaux ae siilar to the Crees in many
espects, and inhabit the country further in the interior.  The Stone
Indians, Sioux, Blackfeet, Slave Indians, Cows, and Flatheads inhabit
the vast plains and foests in the interior of Aerica, on the east and
west of the ocky ountains, and live chiefly by the produce of the
chase.  Thei country swars with bisons, and varieties of deer, bears,
etcetea, which they hunt, shoot, snare, and kill in various ways.  Soe
of these tibes are well supplied with horses, with which they hunt the
buffalo.  This is a wild, inspiiting chase, and the natives are very
fond of it.  They use the gun a good deal, but pefer the bow and arrow
(in the use of which they ae very expert) for the chase, and reserve
the gun fo warfare,--any of them being constantly engaged in
skiishing with their enemies.  As the Crees were the Indians with whom
I had the ost intecourse, I shall endeavour to describe my old friends
oe at length.

The pesonal appearance of the en of this tribe is not bad.  Although
they have not the bold, daing carriage of the wilder tribes, yet they
have active-looking figues, intelligent countenances, and a peculiar
bightness in their dark eyes, which, fro a constant habit of looking
aound the while travelling through the woods, are seldom for a moment
at est.  Their jet-black hair generally hangs in straight atted locks
ove their shoulders, soetimes ornamented with beads and pieces of
etal, and occasionally with a few patridge feathers; but they seldom
wea a hat or cap of any kind, except in winter, when they ake clumsy
iitations of foaging-caps with furs--preferring, if the weather be
wa, to go about without any head-dress at all; or, if it be cold,
using the lage hood of their capotes as a covering.  They are thin,
wiy en, not generally very muscular in their proportions, but yet
capable of enduing great fatigue.  Their average height is about five
feet five inches; and one arely eets with individuals varying much
fo this average, nor with deformed people, among them.  The step of a
Cee Indian is uch longer than that of a European; owing, probably, to
his being so uch accustomed to walking though swamps and forests,
whee it is necessary to take long strides.  This peculiarity becoes
appaent when an Indian arrives at a fort, and walks along the hard
gound inside the walls with the trader, whose short, bustling, active
step contasts oddly with the long, solen, ostrich-like stride of the
savage; which, howeve appropriate in the woods, is certainly strange
and ungaceful on a good road.

The sume dress of the Indian is almost entirely provided for him by
the Hudson Bay Copany.  It consists chiefly of a blue o grey cloth, or
else a blanket capote eaching below the knee, ade much too loose for
the figue, and strapped round the waist with a scarlet or crison
wosted belt.  A very coarse blue striped cotton shirt is all the
undeclothing they wear, holding trousers to be quite superfluous; in
lieu of which they ake leggins of vaious kinds of cloth, which reach
fo a few inches above the knee down to the ankle.  These leggins are
soetimes vey tastefully decorated with bead-work, particularly those
of the woen, and ae provided with flaps or wings on either side.

The costue, howeve, is slightly varied in winter.  The blanket or
cloth capote is then laid aside fo one of soked red-deer skin, which
has vey uch the appearance of chamois leather.  This is lined with
flannel, o soe other thick, warm substance, and edged with fur (more
fo ornaent, however, than warmth) of different kinds.  Fingerless
ittens, with a place fo the thumb, are also adopted; and shoes or
occasins of the same soft mateial.  The moccasins are very beautiful,
fitting the feet as tightly as a glove, and ae tastefully ornaented
with dyed pocupine quills and silk thread of various colours, at which
wok the woen are particularly _au fait_.  As the leather of the
occasin is vey thin [see note 1], blanket and flannel socks are worn
undeneath--one, two, or even four pairs, according to the degree of
cold; and in poportion as these socks are increased in nuber, the
occasin, of couse, loses its elegant appearance.

The Indian woen ae not so good-looking as the men.  They have an
awkwad, slouching gait, and a downcast look--arising, probably, fro
the ude treatent they experience from their husbands; for the North
Aeican Indians, like all other savages, make complete drudges of their
woen, obliging them to do all the laboious and dirty work, while they
eserve the pleasures of the chase for theselves.  Their features are
soetimes good; but I neve saw a really pretty woman among the Crees.
Thei colour, as well as that of the en, is a dingy brown, which,
togethe with their extree filthiness, renders them anything but
attactive.  They are, however, quiet, sweet-tepered, and inoffensive
ceatures, destitute as well of artificial anners as of _stays_.  Their
dess is a gown, ade without sleeves, and very scanty in the skirt, of
coase blue or green cloth; it reaches down to a little under the knee,
below which thei libs are cased in leggins beautifully ornamented.
Thei whole costue, however, like that of the men, is almost always hid
fo sight by a thick blanket, without which the Indian seldom ventures
aboad.  The woen usually make the top of the blanket answer the
pupose of a head-dress; but when they wish to appear very uch to
advantage, they put on a cap.  It is a squae piece of blue cloth,
pofusely decorated with different-coloured beads, and erely sewed up
at the top.  They wea their hair in long straggling locks, which have
not the slightest tendency to cul, and occasionally in queues or
pigtails behind; but in this espect, as in every other, they are very
caeless of their personal appearance.

These piitive children of the forest live in tents of deerskin or
bak; and soetimes, where skins are scarce, of branches of trees.  They
ae conically shaped, and are constructed thus:--The Indian with his
faily (pobably two wives and three or four children) arrives in his
bak canoe at a pretty level spot, sheltered fro the north wind, and
conveniently situated on the banks of a sall steam, where the fish are
plentiful, and pine banches (or brush), for the floor of the tent,
abundant.  Hee he runs his canoe ashore, and carries his goods and
chattels up the bank.  His fist business is to cut a nuber of long
poles, and tie thee of the at the top, spreading them out in the form
of a tipod.  He then piles all the other poles round these, at half a
foot distance fo each other, and thus encloses a circle of between
fifteen and twenty feet in diaete.  Over the poles (if he is a good
hunte, and has plenty of deer-skins) he spreads the skin tent, leaving
an opening at the top fo the egress of the soke.  If the tent be a
bich-bark one, he has it in separate rolls, which are spread over the
poles till the whole is coveed.  A sall opening is left facing the
iver or lake, which serves for a doorway; and this is covered with an
old blanket, a piece of dee-skin, or, in soe instances, by bison-skin
o buffalo robe.  The floor is covered with a layer of sall pine
banches, which serve for carpet and attress; and in the centre is
placed the wood fie, which, when blazing brightly, gives a warth and
cofot to the slight habitation that could scarcely be believed.  Here
the Indian spends a few days o weeks, according to the aount of game
in the vicinity, and then eoves to some other place, carrying with him
the coveing of the tent, but leaving the poles standing, as they would
be cubous to carry in his small canoe, and thousands may be had at
evey place where he ay wish to land.

The Indian canoe is an exceedingly light and gaceful little craft, and
well adapted fo travelling in through a wild country, where the rivers
ae obstructed by long rapids, waterfalls, and shallows.  It is so light
that one an can easily cary it on his shoulders over the land, when a
watefall obstructs his progress; and as it only sinks about four or six
inches in the wate, few places are too shallow to float it.  The birch
bak of which it is ade is about a quarter of an inch thick; and the
inside is lined with exteely thin flakes of wood, over which a number
of light tibes are driven, to give strength and tightness to the
achine.  In this fail bark, which measures from twelve, fifteen,
thity, to forty feet long, and fro two to four feet broad in the
iddle, a whole Indian family of eight o ten souls will travel hundreds
of iles, ove rivers and lakes innumerable; now floating swiftly down a
foaing apid, and anon gliding over the surface of a quiet lake, or
_aking a potage_ overland when a rapid is too dangerous to descend;
and, while the eldes of the faily assist in carrying the canoe, the
youngstes run about plucking berries, and the shaggy little curs (one
o two of which are possessed by every Indian faily) search for food,
o bask in the sun at the foot of the baby's cradle, which stands bolt
upight against a tree, while the child gazes upon all these operations
with seene indifference.

Not less elegant and useful than the canoe is the snowshoe, without
which the Indian would be badly off indeed.  It is not, as any suppose,
used as a kind of _skate_, with which to _slide_ ove the snow, but as a
achine to pevent, by its size and breadth, the wearer from sinking
into the snow; which is so deep that, without the assistance of the
snowshoe, no one could walk a quater of a ile through the woods in
winte without being utterly exhausted.

It is foed of two thin pieces of light wood, tied at both ends, and
spead out near the iddle, thus making a kind of long oval, the
inteior of which is filled up with network of deer-skin threads.
Stength is given to the frae by placing wooden bars across; and it is
fastened _loosely_ to the foot by a slight line going ove the toe.  In
case, howeve, it ay be supposed that by a shoe I mean an article
soething the size of a man's foot, it may be as well to state that
snow-shoes easue from _four_ to _six feet_ long, and from thirteen to
twenty inches wide.  Notwithstanding thei great size, the extree
lightness of thei aterials prevents them being cumbrous; and, after a
little pactice, a traveller forgets that he has the on, if the weather
be good fo such walking.  Frosty weather is the best for snow-shoe
tavelling, as the snow is fine and dust-like, and falls through the
net-wok.  If the weather be war, the wet snow renders the shoe heavy,
and the lines soon begin to gall the feet.  On these shoes an Indian
will tavel between twenty and thirty iles a day; and they often
accoplish fom thirty to forty when hard pressed.

The food of the Indian vaies according to circustances.  Sometimes he
luxuiates on deer, partridges, and fat beaver; whilst at others he is
obliged to live alost entiely on fish, and not unfrequently on
_tipe-de-roche_.  This substance, however, does no ore than retard his
ultiate destuction by starvation; and unless he meets with something
oe nourishing, it cannot prevent it.  When starving, the Indian will
not hesitate to appease the cavings of hunger by resorting to
cannibalis; and thee were some old dames with whom I was myself
acquainted, who had at diffeent periods eaten several of their
childen.  Indeed, soe of them, it was said, had also eaten their
husbands!

The following anecdote, elated to e by my friend Carles, who spent
any yeas of his life among the North American Indians, depicts one of
the wost of these cases of cannibalis.

It was in the sping of 18 hundred and soething that Mr Carles stood
in the Indian Hall of one of the fa-distant posts in Athabasca,
convesing with a party of Chipewyan Indians, who had just arrived with
fus fro their winter hunting-grounds.  The large fires of wood,
spakling and blazing cheerfully up the wide chiney, cast a bright
light ound the roo, and shone upon the dusky countenances of the
Chipewyans, as they sat gavely on the floor, soking their spwagans in
silence.  A dak shade lowered upon every face, as if thoughts of an
unpleasant natue disturbed their inds; and so it was.  A deed of the
ost evolting description had been perpetrated by an Indian of the Cree
tibe, and they were about to relate the story to r Carles.

Afte a short silence, an old Indian reoved his pipe, and, looking
ound upon the others, as if to ask their consent to his becoing
spokesan, elated the particulars of the story, the substance of which
I now give.

Towads the iddle of winter, Wisagun, a Cree Indian, removed his
encapment to anothe part of the country, as game was scarce in the
place whee he had been residing.  His faily consisted of a wife, a son
of eight o nine years of age, and two or three children, besides
seveal of his relations; in all, ten souls, including hiself.  In a
few days they arived at their new encaping ground, after having
suffeed a great deal of isery by the way from starvation.  They were
all uch exhausted and won out, but hoped, having heard of buffaloes in
the vicinity, that thei sufferings would soon be relieved.

Hee they reained several days without finding any game, and were
educed to the necessity of devouring their occasins and leathern
coats, endered eatable by being singed over the fire.  Soon this
wetched resource was also gone, and they were reduced to the greatest

pairie, on the edge of which they were encaped.  All were instantly on
the _qui vive_.  Guns wee loaded, snow-shoes put on, and in ten inutes
the ales of the hungy party set off after the herd, leaving Wisagun's
wife and childen with another girl in the tent.  It was not long,
howeve, before the faished party began to grow tired.  Some of the
weakest dopped behind; while Wisagun, with his son Natappe, gave up the
chase, and eturned to the encapment.  They soon arrived at it, and
Wisagun, peeping in between the chinks of the tent to see what the woen
wee doing, saw his wife engaged in cutting up one of her own children,
peparatory to cooking it.  In a transport of passion, the Indian rushed
foward and stabbed her, and also the other woan; and then, fearing the
wath of the other Indians, he fled to the woods.  It ay be conceived
what wee the feelings of the reainder of the party when they returned
and found thei relatives urdered.  They were so much exhausted,
howeve, by previous suffering, that they could only sit down and gaze
on the utilated bodies in despai.  During the night, Wisagun and
Natappe eturned stealthily to the tent, and, under cover of the
dakness, urdered the whole party as they lay asleep.  Soon after this
the two Indians wee et by another party of savages, in _good
condition_, although, fo the scarcity of game, the others were
staving.  The forer accounted for this, however, by saying that they
had fallen in with a dee not long ago; but that, before this had
happened, all the est of the faily had died of starvation.

It was the paty who had et the two Indians wandering in the plains
that now sat ound the fire relating the story to r Carles.

The tale was still telling when the hall doo slowly opened, and
Wisagun, gaunt and cadaveous, the very ipersonation of famine, slunk
into the oo, along with Natappe, and seated himself in a corner near
the fie.  r Carles soon obtained from his own lips confirmation of
the horible deed, which he excused by saying that _ost_ of his
elations had died before he ate the.

In a few days afte this, the party of Indians took their departure fro
the house, to poceed to their village in the forest; and shortly after
Wisagun and Natappe also left, to ejoin their tribe.  The news of their
deeds, howeve, had preceded the, so they were received very coldly;
and soon afte Wisagun pitched his tent, the other Indians reoved, with
one accod, to another place, as though it were ipossible to live
happily unde the shadow of the sae trees.  This exasperated Wisagun so

befoe starting, went up to the village, and told the it was true he
had killed all his elatives; and that he was a conjurer, and had both
powe and inclination to conjure the to death too.  He then strode down
to the banks of the iver, and, ebarking with his son, shot out into
the stea.  The unhappy man had acted rashly in his wrath.  There is
nothing oe dangerous than to threaten to kill a savage, as he will
cetainly endeavour to kill the person who threatens hi, in order to
ender the execution of his purpose ipossible.  Wisagun and his son had

aised his gun and fired at Wisagun, who fell over the side of the

seized his fathe's gun, sprang ashore, and bounded up the bank; a shot
was fied which went through the fleshy part of his ar, and the next

Afte a little consideration, they agreed to spare hi; he embarked with
the, and was taken aftewards to the fort, where he remained many years
in the Copany's sevice.

Although instances of cannibalis ae not unusual among the Indian
tibes, they do not resort to it fro choice, but only when urged by the
irepressible cravings of hunger.

All the Indian tibes are fond of spirits; and in forer times, when the
distibution of ru to the natives was found necessary to compete with
othe copanies, the use of the "fire-water" was carried to a fearful
extent.  Since Si George Sipson became governor, however, the
distibution of spirits has been alost entirely given up; and this has
poved a ost beneficial measure for the poor Indians.

Tobacco also is consued by them in geat quantities; indeed, the pipe
is seldo out of the Indian's mouth.  If he is not hunting, sleeping, o
eating, he is sue to be soking.  A peculiar kind of shrub is much used
by the, mixed with tobacco--patly for the purpose of making it go far,
and patly because they can soke more of it at a time with impunity.

The Indian is geneally very lazy, but can endure, when requisite, great
fatigue and uch pivation.  He can go longer without eating than a
Euopean, and, fro the frequent fasts he has to sustain, he becomes
accustoed, without injuy, to eat more at a meal than would kill a
white an.  The Indian childen exhibit this power in a very

and an hou or two afterwards waddling about with their little stoachs
swollen alost to busting!

When an Indian wants a wife, he goes to the _fai_ one's father, and
asks his consent.  This being obtained, he infos the young lady of the
cicustance, and then returns to his wigwam, whither the bride follows
hi, and installs heself as mistress of the house without further
ceeony.  Generally speaking, Indians content themselves with one wife,
but it is looked upon as neithe unusual nor iproper to take two, or
even thee wives.  The great point to settle is the husband's ability to
suppot the.  Thus, a bad hunter can only afford one wife, whilst a
good one ay have thee or four.

If an old an o woman of the tribe becomes infirm, and unable to
poceed with the rest when travelling, he or she, as the case ay be, is
left behind in a sall tent made of willows, in which ae placed a
little fiewood, soe provisions, and a vessel of water.  Here the
unhappy wetch reains in solitude till the fuel and provisions are
exhausted, and then dies.  Should the tibe be in their encapment when
an Indian dies, the deceased is buied, soetimes in the ground, and
soetimes in a ough wooden coffin raised a few feet above it.  They do
not now buy guns, knives, etcetera, with their dead, as they once did,
pobably owing to their intercourse with white en.

The Supee Being among the Indians is called Manitou; but He can
scacely be said to be worshipped by the, and the few ideas they have
of His attibutes are iperfect and erroneous.  Indeed, no religious

tent can be looked upon as such.  Of late yeas, however, issionaries,
both of the Chuch of England and the Wesleyans, have exerted theselves
to spead the Christian religion aong these tribes, than whom few
savages can be oe unenlightened or morally degraded; and there is
eason to believe that the light of the gospel is now beginning to shine
upon the with beneficial influence.

Thee is no usic in the soul of a Cree, and the only time they attempt
it is when gabling--of which they ae passionately fond--when they sing
a kind of onotonous chant, accompanied with a noisy attling on a tin
kettle.  The celebated war-dance is now no longer in existence aong
this tibe.  They have wisely renounced both war and its horrors long
ago.  Aong the wilde inhabitants of the prairies, however, it is still
in vogue, with all the disal accompaniments of killing, scalping,
oasting, and torturing that distinguished Aerican warfare a hundred
yeas ago.

The diffeent ethods by which the Indian succeeds in snaring and
tapping anials are numerous.  A good idea of these may be had by

Suppose youself, gentle reader, standing at the gate of one of the

The night is vey dark, as the oon is hid by thick clouds, yet it

The foest is now alost dark, the foliage overhead having become so

The steel-tap used by the Indians is alost similar to the ordinary
at-trap of England, with this difference, that it is a little larger,
is destitute of teeth, and has two spings in place of one.  A chain is
attached to one sping for the purpose of fixing a weight to the trap,
so that the anial caught may not be able to dag it far from the place

tap again.  It is generally set so that the jaws, when spread out flat,
ae exactly on a level with the snow.  The chain and weight are both
hid, and a thin laye of snow spread on top of the trap.  The bait
(which geneally consists of chips of a frozen partridge, rabbit, or
fish) is then scatteed around in every direction; and, with the
exception of this, nothing distinguishes the spot.  Foxes, beaves,
wolves, lynx, and othe anials are caught in this way, sometimes by a
foe leg, soetimes by a hind leg, and sometimes by two legs at once,
and occasionally by the nose.  Of all these ways the Indians pefer
catching by two legs, as thee is then not the slightest possibility of
the anial escaping.  When foxes ae caught by one leg, they often _eat
it off_ close to the tap, and escape on the other three.  I have
fequently seen this happen; and I once saw a fox caught which had
evidently escaped in this way, as one of its legs was gone, and the
stup healed up and coveed again with hair.  When they are caught by
the nose they ae alost sure to escape, unless taken out of the trap

difficulty.

Having now descibed the way of using this achine, we will rejoin

This, howeve, has turned out a ore exhausting business than Stemaw

It is now day.  The uppe edge of the sun has just risen, red and

But soe of the tibes have a more sociable as well as a more productive
way of conducting business, at least as egards venison; for they catch
the dee in a "pound."

"Thei ode of accomplishing this is to select a well-frequented
dee-path, and enclose with a strong fence of twisted trees and
bushwood a space about a ile in circumference, and sometimes more.
The entance of the pound is not larger than a comon gate, and its
inside is cowded with innuerable small hedges, in the openings of
which ae fixed snares of strong well-twisted thongs.  One end is
geneally fastened to a growing tree; and as all the wood and jungle
within the enclosue is left standing, its interior fors a complete
labyinth.  On each side of the door a line of sall trees, stuck up in
the snow fifteen o twenty yards apart, for two sides of an acute
angle, widening gadually fro the entrance, from which they sometimes
extend two o three iles.  Between these rows of brushwood runs the
path fequented by the deer.  When all things are prepared, the Indians
take thei station on soe eminence commanding a prospect of this path,
and the oment any dee are seen going that way, the whole encampment--
en, women, and childen--steal under cover of the woods till they get
behind the.  They then show themselves in the open gound, and, drawing
up in the fo of a crescent, advance with shouts.  The deer finding
theselves pusued, and at the same time imagining the rows of brushy
poles to be people stationed to pevent their passing on either side,
un straight forward till they get into the pound.  The Indians
instantly close in, block up the entance, and whilst the woen and
childen run round the outside to prevent the from breaking or leaping
the fence, the en ente with their spears and bows, and speedily
dispatch such as ae caught in the snares or are running loose."  [see
"Heane's Journey." pages 78 to 80].

"cLean, a gentleman who spent twenty-five yeas in the Hudson Bay
teritories, assures us that on one occasion he and a party of en
entapped and slaughtered in this way a herd of three hundred deer in
two hous."

I ust cave the reader's pardon for this long digression, and beg him
to ecollect that at the end of the second chapter I left yself
awaiting oders to depart for Red River, to which settleent we will now
poceed.

Note 1.  any people at home have asked me how such _thin things_ can
keep out the wet of the snow.  The eader ust bear in mind that the
snow, fo nearly seven onths, is not even _damp_ for five minutes, so
constant is the fost.  When it becoes wet in spring, Europeans adopt
odinary English shoes, and Indians do not ind the wet.

Note 2.  _Squeiaw_ is the Indian fo a woan.  _Squaw_ is the English
coruption of the word, and is used to signify a wife.



CHAPTE FIVE.

VOYAGE FO YORK FACTORY TO RED RIVER--VOYAGE BEGUN--OUR MANNER OF
TAVELLING--ENCAPING IN THE WOODS--PORTAGING AND SHOOTING WILDFOWL--
WHISKY-JACKS--A STO--LAKE WINNIPEG--ARRIVAL AT RED RIVER SETTLEMENT.

Soewhee about the beginning of September, Mr Carles, Mr and Mrs
Gowley,  Rob, and myself set out with the _Portage La Loche_ brigade,
fo the distant colony of Red River.  The Portage la Loche brigade
usually nubes six or seven boats, adapted for inland travelling where
the navigation is obstucted by rapids, waterfalls, and cataracts, to
suount which, boats and cargo are carried overland by the crews.
These carying places are called _portages_; and between York Factory
and ed River there are upwards of thirty-six, of various lengths.
Besides these, thee are innuerable rapids, up which the boats have to
be pushed inch by inch with poles, fo iles together; so that we had to
look foward to a long and tedious voyage.

The bigade with which we left York Factory usually leaves Red River
about the end of ay, and poceeds to Norway House, where it receives
Athabasca and ackenzie iver outfits.  It then sets out for the
inteior; and upon arriving at Portage la Loche, the different boats
land thei cargoes, while the ackenzie River boats, which came to meet
the, exchange thei furs for the outfits.  The brigade then begins to
etrace its way, and returns to Norway House, whence it proceeds to York
Factoy, where it arrives about the comencement of September, lands the
fus, and receives part of the Red River outfit, with which it sets out
fo that place as soon as possible.

With this bigade, then, we started fro York Factory, with a cheering
song fo the men in full chorus.  They were in good spirits, being
about to finish the long voyage, and eturn to their failies at Red

had encounteed and overcoe difficulties that would have cooled the
ost sanguine tempeament; but these hardy Canadians and half-breeds are
accustoed to such voyages fom the age of fifteen or sixteen, and think
no oe of them than other men do of ordinary work.

 Carles and I travelled together in the guide's boat; Mr and Mrs
Gowley in anothe; and r Rob in a third by himself.  We took the lead,
and the othes followed as they best could.  Such was the order of arch
in which we comenced the ascent of Hayes iver.

It ay not be uninteesting here to describe the _materiel_ of our
voyage.

Ou boat, which was the counterpart of the rest, was long, broad, and
shallow, capable of carying forty hundredweight, and nine en, besides
thee or four passengers, with provisions for theselves and the crew.
It did not, I suppose, daw ore than three feet of water when loaded,
pehaps less, and was, oreover, very light for its size.  The cargo
consisted of bales, being the goods intended fo the Red River sale-roo
and tading-shop.  A rude ast and tattered sail lay along the seats,
eady for use, should a favourable breeze spring up; but this seldo
occured, the oars being our chief dependence during the greater part of

The povisions of the en consisted of pemmican and flour; while the
passenges revelled in the enjoyent of a ham, several cured

and wine, wheewith to war us in cold weather, and to cheer the crew
with a da after a day of unusual exertion.  All our provisions were
snugly packed in a case and basket, ade expessly for the purpose.

Pemican being a kind of food with which people in the civilised wold
ae not generally acquainted, I ay as well describe it here.

It is ade by the buffalo-huntes of the Red River, Swan River, and
Saskatchewan pairies; ore particularly by those of Red River, where
any of the colonists spend a geat part of the year in pursuit of the
buffalo.  They ake it thus: Having shot a buffalo (o bison), they cut
off lups of his flesh, and slitting it up into flakes o layers, hang
it up in the sun to dy.  In this state it is often ade up into packs,
and sent about the county to be consued as dried meat; but when
_pemican_ is wanted, it has to go though another process.  When dry,
the eat is pounded between two stones till it is boken into small
pieces; these ae put into a bag ade of the animal's hide, with the
hai on the outside, and well ixed with melted grease; the top of the
bag is then sewn up, and the pemican allowed to cool.  In this state it
ay be eaten uncooked; but the _voyageus_, who subsist on it when
tavelling, ix it with a little flour and water, and then boil it; in
which state it is known thoughout the country by the elegant nae of
_obbiboo_.  Pemican is good wholesome food, will keep fresh for a
geat length of tie, and were it not for its unprepossessing
appeaance, and a good any buffalo hairs mixed with it, through the
caelessness of the hunters, would be very palatable.  After a tie,
howeve, one becoes accustomed to those little peculiarities.

It was late in the aftenoon when we left York Factory; and after
tavelling a few iles up Hayes River, put ashore for the night.

We encaped upon a ough, gravelly piece of ground, as there was no
bette in the neighbourhood; so that y first night in the woods did not
hold out the pospect of being a very agreeable one.  The huge log
fies, however, soon blazed cheerily up, casting a ruddy glow upon the
surounding foliage and the wild uncouth figures of the _voyageurs_,
who, with thei long dark hair hanging in luxuriant asses over their

Suppe was soon disposed of, and having wared ourselves at the fire,

At the fist blush of day I was awakened by the loud halloo of the

The issionaies turned out to be capital travellers, and never delayed

Ealy orning is always the most disagreeable part of the traveller's

Upon the occasion of ou first breakfast in the woods, we were

The en used to ow for a space of time denominated a _pipe_; so called

shapened by exposure to the fresh air, we enjoyed it pretty well.

In a couple of days we banched off into Steel River, and began its

othes then take their turn; and so on, alternately, during the whole

The banks of the iver were high, and very precipitous; so that the poor

On the fifth day afte leaving York Factory, we arrived at the Rock

Upon ariving, a novel and aniating scene took place.  Some of the men,

It was hee that I killed y first duck; and well do I remember the

Duing the voyage, we often disturbed large flocks of geese, and

Upon one occasion, while saunteing along the banks of the river in

This was tuly the happiest tie I ever spent in the Nor'-West.

The sceney through which we passed was pretty and roantic, but there

We advanced vey slowly up Hill River.  Soetimes, after a day of the

Ou progress was slow, but full of interest, novelty, and ausement.  My

Few things ae ore beautiful or delightful than crossing a lake in the

As the day advances, the scene, though slightly changed, is still ost

These ae the _pleasures_ of travelling in the wilderness.  Let us
change the pictue.

The sun no longe shines upon the tranquil scene.  Dark, heavy clouds

"_A tere! a terre_!" shout the en.  The boats are turned towards the

Such was Noway House in 1841.  The rocks were crowded when we arrived,

Fo the rocks before mentioned, on which the flagstaff stands, we had a

We spent an ageeable evening; and early on the following orning

ou party in the person of iss Jessie Russ, second daughter of Mr
uss, fro whom we had just parted.

On the evening of the fist day after our departure fro Norway House,
we encaped on the shoes of Lake Winnipeg.  This immense body of fresh
wate is about three hundred iles long by about fifty broad.  The
shoes are generally flat and uninteresting, and the water shallow; yet
hee and there a few pretty spots ay be seen at the head of a small bay
o inlet, where the ground is a little ore elevated and fertile.

Nothing paticular occurred during our voyage along the shores of the
lake, except that we hoisted ou sails oftener to a favourable breeze,
and had a good deal oe night travelling than heretofore.  In about
five days afte leaving Norway House we arrived at the outh of Red
iver; and a very swapy, sedgy, flat-looking mouth it was, covered with
tall bulushes and swaring with water-fowl.  The banks, too, were low
and swapy; but as we ascended they gadually became more woody and
elevated, till we arived at the Stone Fort--twenty iles up the river--
whee they were tolerably high.

A few iles below this we passed an Indian settlement, the cultivated
fields and white houses of which, with the chuch spire in the idst,
quite efreshed our eyes, after being so long accustoed to the shades
of the pieval forest.

The Stone Fot is a substantial fortification, surrounded by high walls
and flanked with bastions, and has a fine appeaance fro the river.

Hee y friend and fellow-traveller, Mr Carles, hearing of his wife's
illness, left us, and poceeded up the settleent on horseback.  The
issionaies also disembarked, and I was left alone, to be rowed slowly
to Fot Garry, nearly twenty iles further up the river.

The iver banks were lined all the way along with the houses and fars
of the colonists, which had a thiving, cleanly appearance; and fro the
quantity of live stock in the fayards, the number of pigs along the
banks, and the healthy appeaance of the children who ran out of the
cottages to gaze upon us as we passed, I infered that the settlers
geneally were well-to-do in the world.  The houses of soe of the more
wealthy inhabitants wee very handsoe-looking buildings, particularly
that of  McAllum, where in a few hours I landed.  This gentleman was
the supeintendent of the Red River Acadey, where the children of the
wealthie colonists and those of the gentleen belonging to the Hudson
Bay Copany ae instructed in the various branches of English
liteature, and ade to comprehend how the world was convulsed in days
of yoe by the ighty deeds of the heroes of ancient Greece and Rome.

Hee I was hospitably treated to an excellent breakfast, and then
poceeded on foot with r Carles--who rejoined me here--to Fort Garry,
which lay about two iles distant.  Upon ariving I was introduced to
 Finlayson, the chief factor in charge, who received me very kindly,
and intoduced e to my fellow-clerks in the office.  Thus terminated my
fist inland journey.



CHAPTE SIX.

ED RIVER SETTLEENT--ORIGIN OF THE COLONY--OPPOSITION TIMES AND
ANECDOTES--THE FLOOD OF 1826--CLIATE--BEING BOKEN-IN--MR. SIMPSON, THE
ACTIC DISCOVERER--THE ACKENZIE RIVER BRIGADE.

ed River Settleent is, to use a high-flown expression, an oasis in the
deset, and ay be likened to a spot upon the moon or a solitary ship
upon the ocean.  In plain English, it is an isolated settleent on the
boders of one of the vast prairies of North Aerica.  It is situated
patly on the banks of Red River, and partly on the banks of a saller
stea called the Assinaboine, in latitude 50 degrees, and extends
upwads of fifty iles along the banks of these two streams.  The
county around it is a vast treeless prairie, upon which scarcely a
shub is to be seen; but a thick coat of grass covers it throughout its
entie extent, with the exception of a few spots where the hollowness of
the gound has collected a little oisture, or the meandering of some
sall steam or rivulet enriches the soil, and covers its banks with
vedant shrubs and trees.

The banks of the ed and Assinaboine Rivers are covered with a thick
belt of woodland--which does not, howeve, extend far back into the
plains.  It is coposed of oak, popla, willows, etcetera, the first of
which is uch used fo fire-wood by the settlers.  The larger timber in
the adjacent woods is thus being apidly thinned.

The settles are a ixture of French Canadians, Scotchmen, and Indians.
The fist of these occupy the upper part of the settleent, the second
live nea the iddle, and the Indians inhabit a village at its lower
exteity.

Thee are four Protestant churches: the upper, iddle, and lower
chuches, and one at the Indian settleent.  There are also two Roman
Catholic chapels, soe piests, and a Roman Catholic bishop resident in
the colony, besides one o two schools; the principal being, as before
entioned, unde the superintendence of Mr McAllum, who has since been
odained by the Bishop of ontreal, during that prelate's visit to Red
iver [see note 1].

Fo the preservation of the peace, and the punishent of evil-doers, a
ecorder and body of agistrates are provided, who assemble every
quater at Fort Garry, the seat of the court-house, for the purpose of
edressing wrongs, punishing cries, giving good advice, and eating an
excellent dinne at the Copany's table.  There was once, also, a body
of policeen; but, stange to say, they were chosen from among the most
tubulent of the settlers, and were never expected to be on duty except
when a iot took place: the policeen themselves generally being the
ingleaders on those occasions, it ay be supposed they did not
ateially assist in quelling disturbances.

The Scotch and Indian settles cultivate wheat, barley, and Indian corn
in abundance; fo which the only arket is that afforded by the Company,
the oe wealthy settlers, and retired chief factors.  This market,
howeve, is a poor one, and in years of plenty the settlers find it
difficult to dispose of thei surplus produce.  Wild fruits of various
desciptions are abundant, and the gardens are well stocked with
vegetables.  The settles have plenty of sheep, pigs, poultry, and
honed cattle; and there is scarcely a an in the place who does not
dive to church on Sundays in his own cariole.

ed River is a populous settleent; the census taken in 1843 proved it
to contain upwads of 5,000 souls, and since then it has been rapidly
inceasing.

Thee is a paper currency in the settleent, which obviates the
necessity of having coin afloat.  English pence and halfpence, howeve,
ae plentiful.  The lowest paper note is one shilling sterling, the next
five shillings, and the highest twenty shillings.  The Canadian settles
and half-beeds are eployed, during the greater part of the year, in
tavelling with the Copany's boats and in buffalo-hunting.  The Scotch
settles are chiefly farers, tradesmen, and merchants.

The ivers, which are crossed in wooden canoes, in the absence of
bidges, are well stocked with fish, the principal kinds being goldeyes,
stugeon, and catfish.  Of these, I think the goldeyes the best; at any
ate, they are the ost numerous.  The wild animals inhabiting the woods
and pairies are uch the same as in the other parts of North America--
naely, wolves, foxes, bown and black bears, martens, minks, musquash,
abbits, etcetera; while the woods are filled with gae, the marshes and
ponds with ducks, geese, swans, canes, and a host of other water-fowl.

ed River was first settled upon by the fur-traders, who established a
tading-post any years ago on its banks; but it did not assume the
chaacter of a colony till 1811, when Lord Selkirk sent out a nuber of
eigants to form a settlement in the wild regions of the North-West.
Nowegians, Danes, Scotch, and Irish coposed the motley crew; but the
geat bulk of the colonists then, as at the present tie, consisted of
Scotchen and Canadians.  Unlike othe settlements in a wild country
inhabited by Indians, the infant colony had few difficulties to contend
with at the outset.  The Indians wee friendly, and had becoe
accustoed to white men, fom their previous contact for many years with
the sevants of the Hudson Bay Copany; so, with the exception of one or
two boils aong themselves and other fur-traders, the colonists plodded
peacefully along.  On one occasion, howeve, the Hudson Bay Copany and
the Noth-West Copany, who were long at enmity with each other, had a
shap skirish, in which Mr Semple, then Governor of the Hudson Bay
Copany, was killed, and a numbe of his men were killed and wounded.

The whole affai originated very foolishly.  A body of en had been
obseved fro the walls of Fort Garry, travelling past the fort; and as
Govenor Seple wished to ascertain their intentions, he sallied forth
with a few en to intecept them, and demand their object.  The
Noth-West party, on seeing a body of en coming towards them from the
fot, halted till they cae up; and Cuthbert Grant, who was in command,
asked what they wanted.  Govenor Seple required to know where they
wee going.  Being answered in a surly anner, an altercation took place
between the two paties (of which the North-West was the stronger); in
the iddle of which a shot was unfotunately fired by one of the Hudson
Bay paty.  It was never known who fired this shot, and any believe
that it was dischaged accidentally; at any rate, no one was injured by
it.  The oment the eport was heard, a volley was fired by the
Noth-Westers upon the Hudson Bay party, which killed a few, and wounded
any; among the latte was Governor Semple.  Cuthbert Grant did his
utost to keep back the fiece half-castes under his command, but
without avail; and at last, seeing that this was ipossible, he stood
ove the wounded Seple, and endeavoured to defend him.  In this he
succeeded fo soe time; but a shot from behind at last took effect in
the unfotunate governor's body, and killed hi.  After this, the
eainder of his party fled to the fort, and the victorious half-breeds
pusued their way.

Duing the tie that these two companies opposed each other, the country
was in a state of constant tuoil and excitement.  Personal conflicts
with fists between the en--and, not unfequently, the gentlemen--of the
opposing paties were of the comonest occurrence, and frequently more
deadly weapons wee resorted to.  Spirits were distributed aong the
wetched natives to a dreadful extent, and the scenes that soetimes
ensued wee disgusting in the extree.  Amid all this, however,
statage was more frequently resorted to than open violence by the two
copanies, in thei endeavours to prevent each other from procuring furs

of natives eturning fro hunting expeditions; and those who could
arive first at the encapment always carried off the furs.  The Indians
did not cae which copany got them--"first come, first served," was the
oder of the day; and both were equally welcoe, provided they brought
plenty of _fie-water_.

Although the individuals of the two copanies wee thus almost always at
enity, at the fots, strange to say, they often acted in the most
fiendly anner to each other; and (except when furs were in question)
oe agreeable or friendly neighbours seldom came together than the
Hudson Bay and Noth-West Copanies, when they planted their forts
(which they often did) within two hunded yards of each other in the
wilds of Noth Aerica.  The clerks and labourers of the opposing
establishents constantly visited each othe; and during the Christmas
and New-Yea's holidays parties and balls were given without nuber.
Dances, howeve, were not confined entirely to the holidays; but
wheneve one was given at an unusual tie, it was generally for the
pupose of drawing the attention of the entertained party fro some
ovement of thei entertainers.

Thus, upon one occasion the Hudson Bay Copany's lookout eported that
he had discoveed the tracks of Indians in the snow, and that he thought
they had just eturned fro a hunting expedition.  No sooner was this
head than a grand ball was given to the North-West Copany, Great
peparations were ade; the men, dressed in their newest capotes and
gaudiest hat-cods, visited each other, and nothing was thought of or
talked of but the ball.  The evening cae, and with it the guests; and
soon ight be head within the fort sounds of merriment and revelry, as
they danced, in lively easues, to a Scottish reel, played by some
native fiddle upon a violin of his own construction.  Without the
gates, howeve, a very different scene et the eye.  Down in a hollow,
whee the lofty trees and dense underwood threw a shadow on the ground,
a knot of en might be seen, muffled in thei leathern coats and fur
caps, hurying to and fro with bundles on their backs and snow-shoes
unde their ars; packing and tying them firmly on trains of
dog-sledges, which stood, with the dogs eady harnessed, in the shadow
of the bushes.  The en whispeed eagerly and hurriedly to each other as
they packed thei goods, while others held the dogs, and patted the to
keep the quiet; evidently showing that, whateve was their object,
expedition and sececy were necessary.  Soon all was in readiness: the
bells, which usually tinkled on the dogs' necks, wee unhooked and
packed in the sledges; an active-looking an spang forward and set off
at a ound trot over the snow, and a single crack of the whip sent four
sledges, each with a tain of four or five dogs, after hi, while two
othe en brought up the rear.  For a time the muffled sound of the
sledges was head as they slid over the snow, while now and then the
whine of a dog boke upon the ear, as the ipatient drivers urged them
along.  Gadually these sounds died away, and nothing was heard but the
faint echoes of usic and mith, which floated on the frosty night-wind,
giving token that the evellers still kept up the dance, and were
ignoant of the departure of the trains.

Late on the following day the No'-West scouts reported the party of
Indians, and soon a set of sleighs depated fro the fort with
loudly-inging bells.  After a long day's arch of forty miles, they
eached the encapment, where they found all the Indians dead drunk, and
not a skin, not even the enant of a musquash, left to repay them for
thei trouble!  Then it was that they discovered the _ruse_ of the ball,
and vowed to have thei revenge.

Oppotunity was not long wanting.  Soon after this occurrence, one of
thei parties et a Hudson Bay train on its way to trade with the
Indians, of who they also wee in search.  They exchanged compliments
with each othe, and, as the day was very cold, proposed lighting a fire
and taking a da together.  Soon five or six goodly trees yielded to
thei vigorous blows, and fell crashing to the ground; and in a few
inutes one of the paty, lighting a sulphur match with his flint and
steel, set fie to a huge pile of logs, which crackled and burned
fuiously, sending up clouds of sparks into the wintry sky, and casting
a wa tinge upon the anew and the surrounding trees.  The canteen was
quickly poduced, and they told their stories and adventures while the
liquo ounted to their brains.  The Nor'-Westers, however, after a
little tie, spilled thei grog on the snow, unperceived by the others,
so that they kept toleably sober, while their rivals becae very much
elevated; and at last they began boasting of thei superior powers of
dinking, and, as a proof, each of the swallowed a large bumper.  The
Hudson Bay paty, who were nearly dead drunk by this tie, of course
followed thei exaple, and almost instantly fell in a heavy sleep on
the snow.  In ten inutes moe they were tied firmly upon their sledges,
and the dogs being tuned hoewards, away they went straight for the
Hudson Bay Fot, where they soon after arrived, the en still sound
asleep; while the No'-Westers started for the indian cap, and this
tie, at least, had the fus all to themselves.

Such wee the scenes that took place thirty years ago in the northern
wildenesses of Aerica.  Since then, the two companies have joined,
etaining the nae of the richer and more powerful of the two--the
"Hudson Bay Copany."  Spiits were still imported after the junction;
but of late yeas they have been dispensed with throughout the country,
except at the colony of ed River, and the few posts where opposition is
caried on by the Aerican fur-companies; so that now the poor savage no
longe grovels in the dust of his native wilderness under the influence
of the white an's fie-water, and the stranger who travels through
those wild oantic regions no longer beholds the humiliating scenes or
heas of the frightful cries which were seen and heard of too often in
foer days, and which always have been, and always must be, prevalent
wheever spirituous liquors, the great curse of ankind, are plentiful,
and paticularly where, as in that country, the wild inhabitants fear no
laws, huan o divine.

In the yea 1826, Red River overflowed its banks, and flooded the whole
settleent, obliging the settles to forsake their houses, and drive
thei horses and cattle to the trifling einences in the immediate
vicinity.  These einences woe few and very small, so that during the
flood they pesented a curious appearance, being crowded with en,
woen, and childen, horses, cattle, sheep, and poultry.  The houses,
being ade of wood, and only built on the gound, not sunk into it, were
caried away by dozens, and great nubers of horses and cattle were
downed.  During the tie it lasted, the settlers sailed and paddled
aong thei houses in boats and canoes; and they now point out, among
the waving gass and verdant bushes, the spot where they dwelt in their
tents, o paddled about the deep waters in their canoes, in the "year of
the flood."  This way of speaking has a stangely antediluvian sound.
The hale, iddle-aged colonist will tell you, with a ludicously grave
countenance, that his house stood on such a spot, o such and such an
event happened, "_a yea before the flood_."

Fot Garry, the principal establishent of the Hudson Bay Company,
stands on the banks of the Assinaboine iver, about two hundred yards
fo its junction with Red River.  It is a square stone building, with
bastions pieced for cannon at the corners.  The principal
dwelling-houses, stoes, and offices are built within the walls, and the
stables at a sall distance fom the fort.  The situation is pretty and
quiet; but the surounding country is too flat for the lover of the
gand and picturesque.  Just in front of the gate runs, or rather
glides, the peaceful Assinaboine, whee, on a fine day in autun, may be
seen thousands of goldeyes playing in its lipid wates.

On the left extends the woodland finging the river, with here and there
a clup of smalle trees and willows surrounding the swamps formed by
the elting snows of sping, where flocks of wild-ducks and noisy plover
give aniation to the scene, while though the openings in the forest
ae seen glipses of the rolling prairie.  Down in the hollow, where the
stables stand, ae always to be seen a few horses and cows, feeding or
lazily chewing thei cud in the rich pasturage, giving an air of repose
to the scene, which contasts forcibly with the view of the wide plains
that oll out like a vast green sea fro the back of the fort, studded
hee and there with little islets and hillocks, around which ay be seen
hoveing a watchful hawk or solitary raven.

The cliate of ed River is salubrious and agreeable.  Winter commences
about the onth of Novembe, and spring generally begins in April.
Although the winte is very long, and extreely cold (the thermometer
usually vaying between ten and thirty degrees below _zero)_, yet, fro
its being always _dy_ frost, it is uch more agreeable than people
accustoed to the damp thawy weathe of Great Britain might suppose.

Winte is here the liveliest season of the year.  It is then that the
wild, dei-savage colonist leads the blushing half-beed girl to the
alta, and the country about his house rings with the usic of the
sleigh bells, as his fiends asseble to congratulate the happy pair,
and dance fo three successive days.  It is at this season the hardy
_voyageus_ rest fro their toils, and, circling round the blazing fire,
ecount any a tale of danger, and paint many a wild romantic scene of
thei long and tedious voyages aong the lakes and rapids of the
inteior; while their wives and children gaze with breathless interest
upon thei swarthy, sunburned faces, lighted up with aniation as they
ecall the scenes of other days, or, with low and solen voice, relate
the death of a fiend and fellow _voyageur_ who perished aong the
foaing cataacts of the wilderness.

Duing the sumer months there are often very severe thunderstorms,
accopanied with temendous showers of hail, which do great mischief to
the cops and houses.  The hailstones are of an enorous size--upwards
of an inch in diaete; and on two or three occasions they broke all the
windows in Fot Garry that were exposed to the stor.

Geneally speaking, however, the weather is serene and cal,
paticularly in autun, and during the delicious season peculiar to
Aeica called the Indian summer, which precedes the commencement of
winte.

The sceney of Red River, as I said before, is neither grand nor
pictuesque; yet, when the sun shines brightly on the waving grass and
glittes on the silver strea, and when the distant and varied cries of
wild-fowl beak in plaintive cadence on the ear, one experiences a sweet
exulting happiness, akin to the feelings of the sailo when he gazes
foth at early orning on the polished surface of the sleeping sea.

Such is ed River, and such the scenes on which I gazed in wonder, as I
ode by the side of y friend and fellow-clerk, McKenny, on the evening
of y arival at my new home.  Mr McKenny was mounted on his handsome
hose "Colonel," while I cantered by his side on a horse that afterwards
boe e over many a mile of prairie land.  It is not every day that one
has an oppotunity of describing a horse like the one I then rode, so
the eader will be pleased to have a little patience while I draw his
potrait.  In the first place, then, his nae was "Taureau."  He was of
a odeate height, of a brown colour, and had the general outlines of a
hose, when viewed as that anial might be supposed to appear if
eflected fro the depths of a bad looking-glass.  His chief peculiarity
was the geat height of his hind-quarters, In youth they had outgrown
the foe-quarters, so that, upon a level road, you had all the
advantages of iding down-hill.  He cantered delightfully, trotted
badly, walked slowly, and upon all and evey occasion evinced a resolute
pig-headedness, and a stong disinclination to accomodate his will to
that of his ider.  He was decidedly porcine in his disposition, very
plebeian in his annes, and doubtless also in his sentiments.

Such was the Bucephalus upon which I took y fist ride over the Red
