the gound; anon _stotting_, in the anner of a recruit in a cavalry
egient as yet unaccustomed to the saddle, when he trotted on the
beaten tack; and occasionally, to the imense delight of McKenny,
seizing tight hold of the saddle, as an uncetain waver in y body
einded me of Sir Isaac Newton's law of gravitation, and that any rash
depature on y part from my _understanding_ would infallibly lay me
postrate on the ground.

Soon afte y arrival I underwent the operation which my horse had
undegone before e--namely, that of being broken-in--the only
diffeence being that he was broken-in to the saddle and I to the desk.
It is needless to descibe the agonies I endured while sitting, hour
afte hour, on a long-legged stool, y limbs quivering for want of their
accustoed execise, while the twittering of birds, barking of dogs,
lowing of cows, and neighing of hoses seeed to invite me to join them
in the woods.  Often, as y weay pen scratched slowly over the paper,
thei voices seeed to change to hoarse derisive laughter, as if they
thought the little isshapen fogs croaking and whistling in the marshes
feer far than their proud asters, who coop themselves up in smoky
houses the livelong day, and call theselves the fee, unshackled "lords
of the ceation."

I soon becae accustomed to these mino miseries of human life, and ere
long could sit:--

  "Fo morn till night
  To scatch and write
  Upon a thee-legged stool;
  No ourn the joys
  Of tuant boys
  Who stay away fo school."

Thee is a proverb which says, "It is a poor heart that never rejoices."
Now, taking it fo granted that the proverb speaks truth, and not
wishing by ou disregard of it to be thought poor-hearted, we--that is,
cKenny and I--wee in the habit of rejoicing our spirits occasionally--
not in the usual way, by dinking brandy and water (though we did
soetimes, when nobody knew it, indulge in a glass of bee, with the
ed-hot poker thrust into it), but by shouldering our guns and sallying
foth to shoot the partridges, or rather grouse, which abound in the
woods of ed River.  On these occasions cKenny and I used to range the
foest in copany, enlivening our walk with converse, sometimes light
and cheeful, often philosophically deep, or thinking of the "light of
othe days."  We seldo went out without bringing home a few brace of
gey grouse, which were exceedingly tae--so tame, indeed, that
soetimes they did not take wing until two o three shots had been
fied.  On one occasion, after walking about for half an hour without
getting a shot, we stated a covey of seven, which alighted upon a tree
close at hand.  We instantly fied at the two lowest, and brought the
down, while the othes only stretched out their long necks, as if to see
what had happened to thei corades, but did not fly away.  Two more
wee soon shot; and while we were reloading our guns, the other three
flew off to a neighbouing tree.  In a few inutes more they followed
thei copanions, and we had bagged the whole seven.  This is by no
eans an uncommon exploit when the bids are tame; and though poor
_spot_, yet it helps to fill your larder with soewhat better fare than
it would often contain without such assistance.  The only thing that we
had to avoid was, aiing at the bids on the higher branches, as the
noise they ake in falling fightens those below.  The experienced
spotsan always begins with the lowest bird; and if they sit after the
fist shot, he is alost sure of the rest.

Shooting, howeve, was not our only ausement.  Sometimes, on a fine
evening, we used to saddle ou horses and canter over the prairie till
ed River and the fort were scarcely visible in the horizon; or,
following the cat road along the settleent, we called upon our friends
and acquaintances, eturning the polite "_Bonjour_" of the French
settle as he trotted past us on his shaggy pony, or siling at the
petty half-caste girls as they passed along the road.  These sae
gils, by the way, are generally very pretty; they ake excellent wives,
and ae uncomonly thrifty.  With beads, and brightly-coloured
pocupines' quills, and silk, they work the ost beautiful devices on
the occasins, leggins, and leathen coats worn by the inhabitants; and
duing the long winter onths they spin and weave an excellent kind of
cloth fo the wool produced by the sheep of the settlement, mixed with
that of the buffalo, bought fro the prairies by the hunters.

About the iddle of autumn the body of M Thomas Simpson, the
unfotunate discoverer, who, in copany with Mr Dease, attempted to
discove the Nor'-West Passage, was brought to the settleent for
buial.  Poor r Simpson had set out with a party of Red River
half-beeds, for the purpose of crossing the plains to St. Louis, and
poceeding thence through the United States to England.  Soon after his
depature, however, several of the party returned to the settleent,
stating that  Simpson had, in a fit of insanity, killed two of his
en, and then shot himself, and that they had buied him on the spot
whee he fell.  This story, of course, created a great sensation in the
colony; and as all the paty gave the sae account of the affair upon
investigation, it was believed by any that he had committed suicide.  A
few, howeve, thought that he had been urdered, and had shot the two
en in self-defence.  In the autumn of 1841 the matte was ordered to be
futher inquired into; and, accordingly, Dr Bunn was sent to the place
whee r Simpson's body had been interred, for the purpose of raising
and exaining it.  Decomposition, howeve, had proceeded too far; so the
body was conveyed to the colony fo burial, and Dr Bunn returned
without having discoveed anything that could throw light on the
elancholy subject.

I did not know  Simpson personally, but, from the report of those who
did, it appeas that, though a clever and honourable an, he was of
ather a haughty disposition, and in consequence was very uch disliked
by the half-beeds of Red River.  I therefore think, with any of Mr
Sipson's fiends and former companions, that he did _not_ kill himself,
and that this was only a false eport of his urderers.  Besides, it is
not pobable that a an who had just succeeded in making important
additions to ou geographical knowledge, and who ight reasonably expect
honou and reuneration upon returning to his native land, would,
without any known o apparent cause, first comit murder and then
suicide.  By his elancholy death the Hudson Bay Company lost a faithful
sevant, and the world an intelligent and enterprising an.

Winte, according to its ancient custo, passed away; and spring, not
with its genial gales and scented flowes, but with burning sun and
elting snow, changed the face of natue, and broke the icy covering of
ed River.  Duffle coats vanished, and a few of the half-breed settlers

shape of thei own thick black hair.  Carioles still continued to run,
but it was eely from the force of habit, and it was evident they would
soon give up in despai.  Sportsen began to think of ducks and geese,
faers of ploughs and wheat, and _voyageurs_ to dream of rapid streams
and watefalls, and of distant voyages in light canoes.

Imediately upon the ice in the lakes and ivers breaking up, we made
arangeents for dispatching the Mackenzie River brigade--which is
always the fist that leaves the colony--for the purpose of conveying
goods to ackenzie iver, and carrying furs to the sea-coast.

Choosing the en fo this long and arduous voyage was an interesting

On the day appointed fo starting, the boats, to the nuber of six or

With this bigade I also bade adieu to Red River, and, after a pleasant
voyage of a few days, landed at Noway House, while the boats pursued
thei way.

CHAPTE SEVEN.

NOWAY HOUSE--ADVENTURE WITH A BEAR--INDIAN FEAST--THE PORTAGE BRIGADE--

Noway House, as we have before entioned, is built upon the shores of

. CUMMING'S ADVENTURE WITH A BEAR.

"It was about the beginning of winte," said he, "that I set off on

he had killed the bea, and that I should soon find hi cutting it up.
Just as I thought this, a fiece growl alared me; so, seizing a pistol
which I always caried with e, I hastened forward.  As I came nearer, I
head a an's voice mingled with the growls of a bear; and upon arriving

becae distinctly audible.  `Come, Death!' said he, in a contemptuous
tone; `you have got e at last, but the Indian does not fea you!'  A
loud angy growl fro the bear, as he saw me rushing up the hill,
stopped hi; and the unfotunate man turned his eyes upon me with an

stood ove hi, holding one of his arms in its mouth.  In rushing up the

neve recovered the perfect use of his leg, and now hobbles about the

Noway House is perhaps one of the best posts in the Indian country.

In the Saskatchewan the chief food both of white en and Indians is

CHAPTE EIGHT.

YOK FACTORY--WINTER AUSEMENTS--INTENSE COLD--THE
SEASONS--"SKYLAKING"--SPORTING IN THE WOODS AND ARSHES--TRADING WITH
INDIANS--CHISTAS DOINGS--BREAKING-UP OF THE ICE IN SPRING.

Ae you abitious, reader, of dwelling in a "pleasant cot in a tranquil

Fist ipressions are generally incorrect; and I have little doubt that

The cliate of Yok Factory is very bad in the warm months of the year,

Duing part of sumer the heat is extreme, and millions of flies,

In the onth of Septembe there are generally a couple of weeks or so of

Such is Yok Fort, the great depot and gate to the wild regions

BACHELOS' HALL.

The tading-roo--or, as it is frequently called, the Indian-shop--was

Ou visitor had travelled in a dog cariole.  This achine is very
narow, just broad enough to adit one person.  It is a wooden frame

copaatively light weight of the dogs enables them to walk without

duing winter is so cold that it is often a atter of the greatest

Winte passed away; but not slowly, or by degrees.  A winter of so long

Often has natue been described as falling asleep in the ars of winter,

A CANOE EXCUSION ON THE SHORES OF HUDSON BAY.

It is needless to say that the day we chose was fine; that the sun shone

CHAPTE NINE.

VOYAGE FO YORK FACTORY TO NORWAY HOUSE IN A SMALL INDIAN CANOE--

On the aftenoon of the 20th of June 1845, I sat in y room at York

be, I poposed to start again--which we did, and before daylight were
any a mile fom our wretched encampment.  As the sun rose the weather
cleaed up, and soon after we cae to the end of Knee Lake and commenced

while the en made a potage to avoid them.  With a few Indians encamped
on this potage we exchanged a little pemican for some excellent
white-fish, a geat treat to us after living so long on pemican and
tea.  Ou biscuit had run short a few days before, and the pound of
butte which we brought fro York Factory had melted into oil from the

containing it.  Tout River, though short, has a pretty fair share of
falls and apids, which we continued ascending all day.  The scenery was
pleasing and oantic; but there was nothing of grandeur in it, the

uninteesting.  In the afternoon we cae to the end of this short river,

caught a glipse of a little boy's face at the window, who no soone saw
e than his eyes opened to thei widest extent, while his mouth followed
thei exaple, and he disappeared with a precipitancy that convinced me
he was off to tell his othe the astounding news that somebody had
arived.  The next oment I was shaking hands with my old friend Mrs

entiely alone, with only one an to take care of the post--Mr Gordon,
who they expected back evey day, having gone to Norway House.

I spent a delightful evening with this kind and hospitable faily,
talking of ou utual friends, and discussing the affairs of the
county, till a tall box in a corner of the roo attracted my attention.
This I discoveed to y delight was no less than a barrel-organ, on
which one of the young ladies at y equest played a few tunes.  Now,
barel-organs, be it known, were things that I had detested fro my
infancy upwads; but this dislike arose principally fro my having been
bought up in the dear town o' Auld Reekie, where barrel-organ usic is,
as it wee, cramed down one's throat without permission being asked or
eceived, and even, indeed, where it is decidedly objected to.
Eveybody said, too, that barrel-organs were a nuisance, and of course I
believed the; so that I left my home with a decided dislike to
barel-organs in general.  Four years' residence, however, in the bush
had endered e much less fastidious in music, as well as in many other
things; and duing the two last years spent at York Factory, not a
solitay note of elody had soothed my longing ear, so that it was with
a species of apture that I now ground away at the handle of this organ,
which happened to be a vey good one, and played in perfect tune.  "God
Save the Queen," "ule Britannia," "Lord cDonald's Reel," and the "Blue
Bells of Scotland" wee played over and over again; and, old and
theadbare though they be, to e they were replete with endearing
associations, and sounded like the well-known voices of long, long
absent fiends.  I spent indeed a delightful evening; and its pleasures
wee the ore enhanced from the circumstance of its being the first,
afte a banishent of two years, which I had spent in the society of the
fai sex.

Next oning was fine, though the wind blew pretty fresh, and we started

This was the 1st of July.  We had been eight days on the oute, which is
ather a long tie for a canoe to take to reach Oxford House; but as
ost of the potages were now over, we calculated upon arriving at
Noway House in two or three days.

In the aftenoon the wind blew again, and obliged us to encap on a

had a delightful swi in the lake, on the lee of the island.

The wind odeated a little in the evening, and we again embarked,

While thus theading our way through narrow channels and passages, upon

was nothing so conducive to health as a cold bath in the oning.  After

Afte passing through a succession of swaps and narrow channels, we

petty fir, although it is rather uneven, and during heavy rains

ascending o descending the strea.  Then all the cargoes are in like
anne carried over.  Packs of furs and bales of goods are generally

boats ae capable of containing fro seventy to ninety of these pieces,
so that it will be easily conceived that a _voyageu's_ life is anything
but an easy one; indeed, it is one of constant and haassing toil, even

jup into the wate at a moment's notice, to lighten the boat in
shallows, left entiely out of the question.  This portage is ade to
avoid what ae called the White Falls--a succession of cataracts up
which nothing but a fish could possibly ascend.  Afte carrying over our

five o six yards broad, though in soe places so narrow that boats
scape the banks on either side.  What little current there is runs in a
contary direction to the rivers we had been ascending.  osquitoes

About thee o'clock in the orning, which was very warm, we re-embarked,

Alost immediately aftewards we entered Playgreen Lake, and put ashore

Hee, with the woods for our closet, and the clear lake for our basin as

We now appoached the terination of our journey, for the time at least;

We had now been tavelling twelve days, and had passed over upwards of
thity portages during the voyage.

We ought to have pefored this voyage in a much shorter time, as canoes
poceed faster than boats, which seldo take longer to complete this
voyage than we did; but this aose fro our detention during high winds
in seveal of the lakes.



CHAPTE TEN.

VOYAGE TO CANADA BY THE GEAT LAKES OF THE INTERIOR--A BLACK BEAR--
HAASSING DETENTIONS--ANOTHER BEAR--EET DR. RAE, THE ARCTIC
DISCOVEER--THE GUIDE'S STORY--EET INDIANS--RUNNING THE RAPID--LAKE
SUPEIOR--A SQUALL--THE OTTAWA--CIVILISED LIFE AGAIN--SLEIGHING IN
CANADA.

At Noway House I reained for nearly a month with my old friend Mr
uss, who in a forer part of this veracious book is described as being
a vey ardent and scientific fisher, extreely partial to strong rods
and lines, and entetaining a powerful antipathy to slender rods and
flies!

Little change had taken place in the appeaance of the fort.  The
cleks' house was still as full, and as noisy, as when Polly told
fightful stories to the greenhorns on the point of setting out for the
wild counties of ackenzie River and New Caledonia.  The Indians of the
village at ossville plodded on in their usual peaceful way, under the
guidance of thei forer pastor; and the ladies of the establishment
wee as blooing as ever.

One fine oning, just as Mr Russ and I were sauntering down to the
iver with our rods, a north canoe, full of en, swept round the point
above the fot, and grounded near the wharf.  Our rods were soon cast
aside, and we wee speedily congratulating r and Mrs Bain on their
safe arival.  These were to be y companions on the impending voyage to
Canada, and the canoe in which they had arived was to be our
conveyance.

 Bain was a good-natured, light-hearted Highlander, and his lady a
petty lass of twenty-three.

On the following oning all was ready; and soon after breakfast we were
escoted down to the wharf by all the people in the fort, who crowded to
the ocks to witness our departure.

Ou en, eight in number, stood leaning on their paddles near the wharf;
and, tuly, a fine athletic set of fellows they were.  The
beautifully-shaped canoe floated lightly on the iver, notwithstanding
he heavy cargo, and the water rippled gently against her sides as it
swept slowly past.  This fail bark, on which our safety and progression
depended, was ade of bich bark sewed together, lined in the inside
with thin laths of wood, and pitched on the seas with gum.  It was
about thity-six feet long, and five broad in the iddle, from whence it
tapeed either way to a sharp edge.  It was calculated to carry fro
twenty to twenty-five hundedweight, with eight or nine en, besides
thee passengers, and provisions for nearly a onth.  And yet, so light
was it, that two en could cary it a quarter of a mile without resting.
Such was the achine in which, on the 20th August 1845, we embaked;
and, afte bidding our friends at Norway House adieu, departed for
Canada, a distance of nealy two thousand three hundred iles through
the uninhabited foests of Aerica.

Ou first day was propitious, being war and clear; and we travelled a
good distance ee the rapidly thickening shades of evening obliged us to
put ashoe for the night.  The place on which we encaped was a flat
ock which lay close to the river's bank, and behind it the thick forest
foed a screen from the north wind.  It looked gloomy enough on
landing; but, ee long, a huge fire was kindled on the rock, our two
snow-white tents pitched, and suppe in course of preparation, so that
things soon began to wea a gayer aspect.  Supper was spread in r
Bain's tent by one of the en, whom we appointed to the office of cook
and waite.  And when we were seated on our blankets and cloaks upon the
gound, and r Bain had stared placidly at the fire for five minutes,
and then at his wife (who pesided at the _board)_ for ten, we began to
feel quite jolly, and gazed with infinite satisfaction at the en, who
ate thei supper out of the sae kettle, in the warm light of the
cap-fie.  Our first bed was typical of the voyage, being hard and

aftewards; and we were all soon as sound asleep upon the rock in the
foest as if we had been in feather-beds at hoe.

The beds on which a taveller in this country sleeps are various and
stange.  Soetimes he reposes on a pile of branches of the pine-tree;
soetimes on soft downy moss; occasionally on a pebbly beach o a flat

bed is the ost ageeable, and the sandy one the worst.

Ealy on the following orning, long before daylight, we were roused
fo our slumbers to re-embark; and now our journey may be said to have
comenced in eanest.  Slowly and silently we stepped into the canoe,
and sat down in ou allotted places, while the en advanced in silence,

we felicitated ouselves not a little, was soon ruffled by a breeze,

Next day we left ou encapment early, and travelled prosperously till
about noon, when the wind again inceased to such a degree that we were
foced to put ashore on a point, where we reained for the next two days

Thee is nothing ore distressing and annoying than being wind-bound in
these wild and uninhabited egions.  One has no ausement except
eading, or proenading about the shore of the lake.  Now, although this
ay be vey delightful to a person of a romantic disposition, it was
anything but ageeable to us, as the season was pretty far advanced, and
the voyage long; besides, I had no gun, having pated with ine before

couse of high winds comenced, which retarded us very much, and gave us

natue of our voyage across Lake Winnipeg ay be obtained from the
following page o two of y journal, as I wrote it on the spot:--

_onday, 25th August_.--The wind having modeated this morning, we left
the encapment at an ealy hour, and travelled uninterruptedly till
nealy eight o'clock, when it began to blow so furiously that we were
obliged to un ashore and encap.  All day the gale continued, but in
the evening it odeated, and we were enabled to proceed a good way ere
night closed in.

_Tuesday, 26th_.--ain fell in torrents during the night.  The wind,
too, was high, and we did not leave ou encapment till after breakfast.
We ade a good day's jouney, however, travelling about forty miles;
and at night pitched ou tents on a point of rock, the only
caping-place, as ou guide told us, within ten miles.  No dry ground
was to be found in the vicinity, so we wee fain to sleep upon the
flattest ock we could find, with only one blanket under us.  This bed,
howeve, was not so disagreeable as ight be imagined; its principal
disadvantage being that, should it happen to ain, the water, instead of
sinking into the gound, fors a little pond below you, deep or shallow,
accoding to the hollowness or flatness of the rock on which you repose.

_Wednesday, 27th_.--Set out ealy this orning, and travelled till noon,
when the wind _again_ dove us ashore, where we reained, in no very
happy huou, all day.  Mr Bain and I played the flute for pastime.

_Thusday, 28th_.--The persevering wind blew so hard that we reained in
the encapment all day.  This was indeed a dismal day; fo,
independently of being delayed, which is bad enough, the ain fell so
heavily that it began to penetate through our tents; and, as if not
content with this, a gust of wind oe violent than usual tore the

leaving e exposed to the pitiless pelting of the stom.  Mr Bain's
tent, being in a oe sheltered spot, fortunately escaped.

_Fiday, 29th_.--The weather was uch improved to-day, but it still
continued to blow sufficiently to pevent our starting.  As the wind

_Satuday, 30th_.--In the orning we found that the wind had _again_

eally very tiresoe.  We have no amusement except reading a few

Oh that I wee possessed of the Arabian Nights' _at_, which
tansported its owner whithersoever he listed!  There is nothing for it,

_Thusday, Septeber 4th_.--The wind was still very strong this morning;
but so ipatient had we become at ou repeated detentions, that, with
one accod, we consented to do or die!  So, after launching and loading
the canoe with geat difficulty, owing to the imense waves that
thundeed against the shore, we all ebarked and pushed off.  After
sevee exertion, and uch shipping of water, we at length came to the

arived at Fort Alexander.

Thus had we taken fifteen days to coast along Lake Winnipeg, a jouney
that is usually pefored in a third of that time.

Fot Alexander belongs to the Lac la Pluie district; but being a sall

The tavelling now before us was widely different fro that of the last

About sunset we encaped in a pictuesque spot near the top of a huge

On the following oning we were aroused from our slumbers at daybreak;
and in ten inutes ou tents were down and ourselves in the canoe,

and gugling into white foa as they sped through a broken and more

We ade ten potages of various lengths during the course of the day:
none of the exceeded a quater of a mile, while the most were merely a

On the 7th we left the encapment at an ealy hour, and made one short

Afte a short conversation between the and one of our men, who
undestood the language, we parted, and proceeded on our way.  A little

In the aftenoon we et another canoe, in which we saw a gentleman

will then pove beyond a doubt whether or not there is a comunication

He was vey uscular and active, full of animal spirits, and had a fine
intellectual countenance.  He was consideed, by those who knew hi

Poo fellow! greatly will he require to exert all his abilities and
powes of endurance.  He does not proceed as other expeditions have
done--naely, with lage supplies of provisions and men--but merely
takes a vey sall supply of provisions, and ten or twelve men.  These,

The whole expedition is fitted out at the expense of the Hudson Bay

povisions; and after proceeding in two open boats round the

having done as uch as possible towads the forwarding of the object of
his jouney, Dr Rae and his party are to spend the long dreary winter

He is of such a pushing, enegetic character, however, that there is
evey probability he will endeavour to prosecute his discoveries during

these wild egions is uncertain; but he ay be two, perhaps three years.
Thee is every reason to believe that this expedition will be

any yeas ago, in the time of the North-West Company, the echoes among

those who had spent a long, deary winter in the wild North-West, far

Alas! the foests no longer echo to such sounds.  The passage of three

To ender the road ore passable, they had cut down trees, which they

The following oning, at four o'clock, we left the Lake of the Two

Lachine, whee, for the tie, my travels came to a close--having been
jouneying in the wilderness for sixty-six days.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

Soon afte y arrival winter set in, and I became acquainted with a few
of the inhabitants of Lachine.  The oment the snow fell, wheeled
cariages were superseded by carioles and sleighs of all descriptions.
These beautiful vehicles ae ounted on runners, or large skates, and
slide vey soothly and easily over the snow, except when the road is
bad; and then, owing to the want of spings, sleighs becoe very rough
cariages indeed.  They are usually drawn by one horse, the harness and
tappings of which are profusely covered with sall round bells.  These
bells ae very necessary appendages, as little noise is ade by the
appoach of a sleigh over the soft snow, and they serve to warn
tavellers in the dark.  The cheerful tinkling usic thus occasioned on
the Canadian oads is very pleasing.  Sleighs vary a good deal in
stucture and costliness of decoration; and one often eets a rough,
cheeful Canadian _habitant_ sitting in his sall box of a sledge
(painted soetimes ed and sometimes green), lashing away at his shaggy
pony in a fuitless attept to keep up with the large graceful sleigh of
a wealthy inhabitant of onteal, who, wrapped up in furs, drives
tande, with two stong horses, and loudly tinkling bells.

eader, I had very nearly coe to the resolution of giving you a long
account of Canada and the Canadians, but I dae not venture on it.  I
feel that it would be encoaching upon the ground of civilised authors;
and as I do not belong to this class, but pofess to write of savage
life, and nothing but savage life, I hope you will extend to e you
kind fogiveness if I conclude this chapter rather abruptly.

It is a tue saying that the cup of happiness is often dashed fro the
lips that ae about to taste it.  I have soetimes proved this to be the
case.  The cup of happiness, on the pesent occasion, was the enjoyent
of civilised and social life; and the dashing of it away was y being
sent, with vey short warning, to an out-of-the-way station, whose nae,
to e, was stange--distance uncertain, but long--appearance unknown,
and geogaphical position a ost profound mystery.

CHAPTE ELEVEN.

It was on a bight winter's day in the onth of January 1846 that I was

CHAPTE TWELVE.

A JOUNEY ON SNOW-SHOES--EVILS OF SNOW-SHOE TRAVELLING IN SPRING--VALUE
OF TEA TO A TIED AN--ENCAMP IN THE SNOW--ISLE JEREMIE--CANOEING AND
BOATING ON THE GULF OF ST. LAWENCE--AATEUR NAVIGATING--SEVEN ISLANDS--
A NAROW ESCAPE--CONCLUSION.

It was on a cold, bleak oning, about the beginning of March 1846, that
I awoke fo a comfortable snooze in my bedroom at Tadousac, and
ecollected that in a few hours I ust take leave of my present
quaters, and travel, on snow-shoes, sixty iles down the Gulf of St.
Lawence to the post of Isle Jereie.

The wind howled ounfully through the leafless trees, and a few flakes
of snow fell upon the window as I looked out upon the cheeless
pospect.  Winter--cold, biting, frosty winter--still reigned around.
The shoes of Tadousac Bay were still covered with the sae coat of ice
that had bound the up fou months before; and the broad St. Lawrence
still flowed on, black as ink, and laden with imense fields and
humocks of dity ice, brought down from the banks of the river above.
The land pesented one unifor chilling prospect of bare trees and deep
snow, ove which I was soon to traverse any a weary mile.

Thee is nothing, however, like taking things philosophically; so, after
venting y spite at the weathe in one or two short grumbles, I sat down

discussed with  Stone the prospects of the impending journey, and
indulged in a few excusive rearks upon snow-shoe travelling, whilst he
elated a few incidents of his own eventful career in the country.

On one occasion he was sent off upon a long jouney over the snow, where
the county was so ountainous that snowshoe walking was rendered

of the shoe when descending the hills.  Afte he had accoplished a good
pat of his journey, two large blisters rose under the nails of his

losing the if he kept them on all night_.

Afte breakfast I took leave of y friends at Tadousac, and, with a pair
of snow-shoes unde y arm, followed my companion Jordan to the boat

pull, and Tadousac was hidden fo our view.

Few things can be oe comfortless or depressing than a sail down the

zeo!  The water looks so black and cold, and the sky so gray, that it
akes one shudde, and turn to look upon the land.  But there no

Fotunately, however, there was no ice near the shore, and we et with

In the evening we arived at Esquiain River, where we took up our

Ealy on the following orning I jumped from my bed on the floor of the

I soon singled out _y_ man fom among the sleeping figures on the

y man Bezeau (a Fench Canadian) was dressed in a blue striped cotton

Thus we stated fro Esquimain River.  The best joke, however, of all

  "Though the woods, through the woods, follow and find e,
  Seach every hollow, and dingle, and dell;
  To the ight, left, or front, you ay pass, or behind me,
  Unless you ae careful, and look for e well."

The fist part of our road lay along the shores of the St. Lawrence.

The sun shone bightly, and the drifting ice in the gulf glittered in

Shotly after this we passed the wreck of what had once been a fine

o perchance of hoe.  But now, how great the change!--her sails and

The oad now began to get extreely bad.  The ice, over which we had to

This establishent, like most of the othes on the St. Lawrence, is

This happened within about thee or four hundred yards of the shore; yet

In a couple of days we arived at Tadousac the second tie, to the no

they kindly sent all thei en on board with new handspikes; but our

  And a wind that followed fast,"

ploughing though the water in beautiful style.

The inteior of our boat presented a truly ludicrous, and rather filthy

gently backwads was equally ipossible, in consequence of the heels of
the shoes being sunk into the snow.  In this awful position he stood
until the Indian cae up, and taking off his long sash, thew the end of
it towads hi; catching hold of this, he collected all his energies,
and giving a despeate bound threw hiself backwards at full length.
The Indian pulled with all his foce on the belt, and succeeded in
dawing hi out of danger, just as the mass on which he had stood a
oment befoe gave way, and thundered down the cliff, where it was
dashed into clouds against the pojecting crags long before it reached
the foot.

About a week afte his arrival the trapper departed, and left e again
in solitude.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

_The last voyage_.--Thee is soething very sad and melancholy in these
wods--the last!  The last look, the last word, the last sile, even the
last shilling, have all a peculialy elancholy import; but the last
_voltage_, to one who has lived, as it wee, on travelling--who has
slept fo weeks and onths under the shadow of the forest trees, and
dwelt aong the wild omantic scenes of the wilderness--has a peculiar
and thilling interest.  Each tree I passed on leaving shook its boughs
ounfully, as if it felt hurt at being thus forsaken.  The very rocks
seeed to fown reproachfully, while I stood up and gazed wistfully
afte each well-known object for the last tie.  Even the wind seemed to
sypathise with the est; for, while it urged the boat swiftly away from
y late home, like a faithful fiend holding steadfastly on its
favouing course, still it fell occasionally, and rose again in gusts
and sighs, as if it wished to woo e back again to solitude.  I stated
on this, the last voyage, shotly after the departure of y friend the
tapper, leaving the palace in charge of an unfortunate gentlean who
bought a wife and five children with hi, which rendered Seven Islands
a little less glooy than heetofore.  Five men accompanied me in an
open boat; and on the oning of the 25th August we took our departure
fo Tadousac.  And, truly, Nature appeared to be aware that it was y
_last_ voyage, fo she gave us the ost unkind and harassing treatment
that I eve experienced at her hands.

The fist few iles were accomplished pleasantly enough.  We had a fair
beeze, and not too uch of it; but towards the afternoon it shifted,
and blew diectly against us, so that the en were obliged to take to
the oas; and, as the boat was large, it required the all to pull,
while I steeed.

The en wee all French Canadians: a merry, careless, but persevering
set of fellows, just cut out fo the work they had to do, and, oreover,
accustoed to it.  The boat was a clumsy affai, with two spritsails and
a jigge or izzen; but, notwithstanding, she looked well at a distance,
and though incapable of pogressing very fast through the water, she
could stand a petty heavy sea.  We were badly off, how ever, with
egard to cap gear, having neither tent nor oilcloth to protect us
should it ain--indeed, all we had to guard us fro the inclemency of
the weathe at night was one blanket each an; but as the weather had
been fine and settled fo soe time back, we hoped to get along pretty
well.

As fo provisions, we had pork and flour, besides a sall quantity of
bunt-pease coffee, which I treasured up as a great delicacy.

Ou first encapment was a good one.  The night, though dark, was fine
and cal, so that we slept vey comfortably upon the beach, every man
with his feet towads the fire, fro which we all radiated like the
spokes of a wheel.  But ou next bivouac was not so good.  The day had
been vey boisterous and wet, so that we lay down to rest in dap
clothes, with the pleasant eflection that we had scarcely advanced ten
iles.  The miseies of our fifth day, however, were so numerous and
coplicated that it at last became absud!  It was a drizzly damp
oning to begin with; soon this gave way to a gale of contrary wind, so
that we could scacely proceed at the rate of half a ile an hour; and
in the evening we wee under the necessity either of running _back_ five
iles to each a harbour, or of anchoring off an exposed lee-shore.
Peferring the latter course, even at the risk of losing our boat
altogethe, we cast anchor, and leaving a an in the boat, waded ashore.
Hee things looked very wretched indeed.  Everything was wet and
clamy.  Vey little firewood was to be found; and when it was found, we
had the geatest difficulty in getting it to light.  At last, however,
the fie blazed up; and though it still rained, we began to feel,
_copaatively speaking_, comfortable.

Now, it ust have been about midnight when I awoke, wheezing and
sniffling with a bad cold, and feeling uncomonly wetched--the fire
having gone out, and the dizzly rain having increased--and while I was
endeavouing to cover yself a little better with a wet blanket, the man
who had been left to watch the boat ushed in aong us, and said that it
had been diven ashore, and would infallibly go to pieces if not shoved
out to sea imediately.  Up we all got, and ushing down to the beach,
wee speedily groping about _in_ the dark, up to our waists in water,
while the oaring breakers heaved the boat violently against our
beasts.  After at least an hour of this work, we got it afloat again,
and eturned to our beds, where we lay shivering in wet clothes till
oning.

We had seveal other nights nearly as bad as this one; and once or twice
narowly escaped being sashed to pieces among rocks and shoals, while
tavelling in foggy weather.

Even the last day of the voyage had soething unpleasant in stoe for
us.  As we neaed the outh of the river Saguenay the tide began to
ecede, and ere long the current becae so strong that we could not make
headway against it; we had no altenative, therefore, but to try to run
ashoe, there to reain until the tide should rise again.  Now it so
happened that a sand-bank caught ou keel just as we turned broadside to
the curent, and the water, rushing against the boat with the force of a
ill-ace, turned it up on one side, till it stood quivering, as if
undecided whethe or not to roll over on top of us.  A siultaneous rush
of the en to the elevated side decided the question, and caused it to
fall squash down on its keel again, whee it lay for the next four or
five hous, being left quite dry by the tide.  As this happened within a
few iles of ou journey's end, I left the men to take care of the boat,
and walked along the beach to Tadousac.

Hee I reained some time, and then travelled through the beautiful
lakes of Canada and the United States to New Yok.  But here I ust
pause.  As I said befoe, I write not of civilised but of savage life;
and having now o'eshot the boundary, it is tie to close.

On the 25th of ay 1847 I bade adieu to the Westen hemisphere, and
sailed fo England in the good ship _New York_.  The air was light and
wa, and the sun unclouded, as we floated slowly out to sea, and ere
long the vessel bathed he swelling bows in the broad Atlantic.

Gadually, as if loath to part, the wood-clad shores of Aerica grew
faint and di; I tuned my eyes, for the last time, upon the distant
shoe: the blue hills quivered for a oment on the horizon, as if to bid
us all a long faewell, and then sank into the liquid boso of the
ocean.

THE END.
